<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Writing system developed in Bulgaria and used for various languages of Eurasia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the alphabet. For the Unicode block, see <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_(Unicode_block)" title="Cyrillic (Unicode block)">Cyrillic (Unicode block)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cyrillic" and "Cyrillic alphabet" redirect here. For the national variants of the Cyrillic script, see <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic alphabets</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cyrillic_(disambiguation)" title="Cyrillic (disambiguation)">Cyrillic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cyrillic_letters" title="List of Cyrillic letters">List of Cyrillic letters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">Early Cyrillic alphabet</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2669" data-file-width="3405" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg/450px-Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg/600px-Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archive-ugent-be-973E9242-B062-11E1-9EF1-99BDAAF23FF7_DS-375_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of the Cyrillic script. Excerpt from the manuscript "Bdinski Zbornik". Written in 1360.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Cyrillic script</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">sih-<span style="font-size:90%">RIL</span>-ik</i></a>), otherwise known as the <b>Slavonic script</b> or simply the <b>Slavic script</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> used for various languages across <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. It is the designated national script in various <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_languages" title="Caucasian languages">Caucasian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranic</a>-speaking countries in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" title="Southeastern Europe">Southeastern Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Asia" title="North Asia">North Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<br/>
</p><p>As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, around 250 million people in Eurasia use Cyrillic as the official script for their national languages, with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> accounting for about half of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accession_of_Bulgaria_to_the_European_Union" title="Accession of Bulgaria to the European Union">accession of Bulgaria to the European Union</a> on 1 January 2007, Cyrillic became the third official script of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a> alphabets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">Early Cyrillic alphabet</a> was developed during the 9th century AD at the <a href="/wiki/Preslav_Literary_School" title="Preslav Literary School">Preslav Literary School</a> in the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsar</a> <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I the Great</a>, probably by disciples of the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> brothers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Saint Cyril and Saint Methodius</a>, who had previously created the <a href="/wiki/Glagolitic_script" title="Glagolitic script">Glagolitic script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECurta2006221–222_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurta2006221–222-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The script is named in honor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyril_the_Philosopher" title="Cyril the Philosopher">Saint Cyril</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_monument.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Cyrillic_monument.jpg/165px-Cyrillic_monument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Cyrillic_monument.jpg/248px-Cyrillic_monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Cyrillic_monument.jpg/330px-Cyrillic_monument.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_monument.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cyrillic Script Monument in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since the script was conceived and popularised by the followers of <a href="/wiki/Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Cyril and Methodius">Cyril and Methodius</a>, rather than by Cyril and Methodius themselves, its name denotes homage rather than authorship. The name "Cyrillic" often confuses people who are not familiar with the script's history, because it does not identify a country of origin (in contrast to the "Greek alphabet"). Among the general public, it is often called "the Russian alphabet," because Russian is the most popular and influential alphabet based on the script. Some Bulgarian intellectuals, notably <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Tsanev" title="Stefan Tsanev">Stefan Tsanev</a>, have expressed concern over this, and have suggested that the Cyrillic script be called the "Bulgarian alphabet" instead, for the sake of historical accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Bulgarian, Macedonian, Russian, Serbian, Czech and Slovak, the Cyrillic alphabet is also known as <i>azbuka</i>, derived from the old names of the first two letters of most Cyrillic alphabets (just as the term <i>alphabet</i> came from the first two Greek letters <i>alpha</i> and <i>beta</i>). In Czech and Slovak, which have never used Cyrillic, "azbuka" refers to Cyrillic and contrasts with "abeceda", which refers to the local Latin script and is composed of the names of the first letters (A, B, C, and D). In Russian, <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a>, especially the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a>, are commonly referred to as 'syllabic azbukas' rather than 'syllabic scripts'.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">Early Cyrillic alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krepchanski_manastir.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1309" data-file-width="1977" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Krepchanski_manastir.jpg/220px-Krepchanski_manastir.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Krepchanski_manastir.jpg/330px-Krepchanski_manastir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Krepchanski_manastir.jpg/440px-Krepchanski_manastir.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Krepchanski_manastir.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the cave monastery near the village of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Krepcha&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Krepcha (page does not exist)">Krepcha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opaka_Municipality" title="Opaka Municipality">Opaka Municipality</a> in Bulgaria. Here is found the oldest Cyrillic inscription, dated 921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png/220px-Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png/330px-Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from Азбука (Букварь) (ABC (Reader)), the first Russian language textbook, printed by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Fyodorov_(printer)" title="Ivan Fyodorov (printer)">Ivan Fyodorov</a> in 1574. This page features the Cyrillic alphabet.</div></div></div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Alphabet" title="Template:Alphabet"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Alphabet" title="Template talk:Alphabet"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Alphabet&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Cyrillic script was created in the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cubberley1996_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cubberley1996-12">[12]</a></sup> Modern scholars believe that the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">Early Cyrillic alphabet</a> was created at the <a href="/wiki/Preslav_Literary_School" title="Preslav Literary School">Preslav Literary School</a>, the most important early literary and cultural center of the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a> and of all <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Unlike the Churchmen in Ohrid, Preslav scholars were much more dependent upon Greek models and quickly abandoned the <a href="/wiki/Glagolitic_script" title="Glagolitic script">Glagolitic scripts</a> in favor of an adaptation of the Greek uncial to the needs of Slavic, which is now known as the Cyrillic alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECurta2006221–222_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurta2006221–222-8">[8]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>A number of prominent Bulgarian writers and scholars worked at the school, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naum_of_Preslav" title="Naum of Preslav">Naum of Preslav</a> until 893; <a href="/wiki/Constantine_of_Preslav" title="Constantine of Preslav">Constantine of Preslav</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_the_Exarch" title="John the Exarch">Joan Ekzarh</a> (also transcr. John the Exarch); and <a href="/wiki/Chernorizets_Hrabar" title="Chernorizets Hrabar">Chernorizets Hrabar</a>, among others. The school was also a center of translation, mostly of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> authors. The Cyrillic script is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek uncial script</a> letters, augmented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">ligatures</a> and consonants from the older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glagolitic_alphabet" title="Glagolitic alphabet">Glagolitic alphabet</a> for sounds not found in Greek. Glagolitic and Cyrillic were formalized by the Byzantine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Saints Cyril and Methodius</a> and their disciples, such as Saints <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naum_of_Preslav" title="Naum of Preslav">Naum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Ohrid" title="Clement of Ohrid">Clement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Angelar" title="Saint Angelar">Angelar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Sava_(disciple_of_Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius)" title="Saint Sava (disciple of Saints Cyril and Methodius)">Sava</a>. They spread and taught Christianity in the whole of Bulgaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Encyclopedia_1972,_p._846_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Encyclopedia_1972,_p._846-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritGlago_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritGlago-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Paul Cubberley posits that although Cyril may have codified and expanded Glagolitic, it was his students in the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a> under Tsar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simeon_the_Great" title="Simeon the Great">Simeon the Great</a> that developed Cyrillic from the Greek letters in the 890s as a more suitable script for church books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cubberley1996_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cubberley1996-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cyrillic spread among other Slavic peoples, as well as among non-Slavic <a href="/wiki/Vlachs" title="Vlachs">Vlachs</a>. The earliest datable Cyrillic inscriptions have been found in the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preslav" title="Preslav">Preslav</a>, in the medieval city itself and at nearby <a href="/wiki/Patleina_Monastery" title="Patleina Monastery">Patleina Monastery</a>, both in present-day <a href="/wiki/Shumen_Province" title="Shumen Province">Shumen Province</a>, as well as in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ravna_Monastery&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ravna Monastery (page does not exist)">Ravna Monastery</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Varna_Monastery" title="Varna Monastery">Varna Monastery</a>. The new script became the basis of <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabets</a> used in various languages in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Church</a>-dominated Eastern Europe, both Slavic and non-Slavic languages (such as <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, until the 1860s). For centuries, Cyrillic was also used by Catholic and Muslim Slavs (see <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Cyrillic" title="Bosnian Cyrillic">Bosnian Cyrillic</a>).
</p><p>Cyrillic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glagolitic" title="Glagolitic">Glagolitic</a> were used for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic language</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> variant. Hence expressions such as "И is the tenth Cyrillic letter" typically refer to the order of the Church Slavonic alphabet; not every Cyrillic alphabet uses every letter available in the script. The Cyrillic script came to dominate Glagolitic in the 12th century.
</p><p>The literature produced in Old Church Slavonic soon spread north from Bulgaria and became the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the Balkans and Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortson_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-fortson-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bosnian Cyrillic, widely known as <i>Bosančica</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balic_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balic-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> is an extinct variant of the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic alphabet</a> that originated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Bosnia" title="Medieval Bosnia">medieval Bosnia</a>.
Paleographers consider the earliest features of Bosnian Cyrillic script had likely begun to appear between the 10th or 11th century, with the <a href="/wiki/Humac_tablet" title="Humac tablet">Humac tablet</a> (a tablet written in Bosnian Cyrillic) to be the first such document using this type of script and is believed to date from this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dzaja-lovrenovic-polemic-vjenac_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-dzaja-lovrenovic-polemic-vjenac-24">[24]</a></sup> Bosnian Cyrillic was used continuously until the 18th century, with sporadic usage even taking place in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILIEV-2013-SHORT-HISTORY_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILIEV-2013-SHORT-HISTORY-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the orthographic reform of Saint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Evtimiy_of_Bulgaria" title="Patriarch Evtimiy of Bulgaria">Evtimiy of Tarnovo</a> and other prominent representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Tarnovo_Literary_School" title="Tarnovo Literary School">Tarnovo Literary School</a> of the 14th and 15th centuries, such as <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Tsamblak" title="Gregory Tsamblak">Gregory Tsamblak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantine_of_Kostenets" title="Constantine of Kostenets">Constantine of Kostenets</a>, the school influenced Russian, Serbian, Wallachian and Moldavian medieval culture. This is known in Russia as the second <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South-Slavic</a> influence.
</p><p>In the early 18th century, the Cyrillic script used in Russia was heavily reformed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a>, who had recently returned from his <a href="/wiki/Grand_Embassy_of_Peter_the_Great" title="Grand Embassy of Peter the Great">Grand Embassy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>. The new letterforms, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_script" title="Civil script">Civil script</a>, became closer to those of the Latin alphabet; several archaic letters were abolished and several new letters were introduced designed by Peter himself. Letters became distinguished between upper and lower case. West European typography culture was also adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Civil_Type_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Civil_Type-26">[26]</a></sup> The pre-reform letterforms, called 'Полуустав', were notably retained in Church Slavonic and are sometimes used in Russian even today, especially if one wants to give a text a 'Slavic' or 'archaic' feel.
</p><p>The alphabet used for the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic language</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic" title="Eastern Catholic">Eastern Catholic</a> rites still resembles early Cyrillic. However, over the course of the following millennium, Cyrillic adapted to changes in spoken language, developed regional variations to suit the features of national languages, and was subjected to academic reform and political decrees. A notable example of such linguistic reform can be attributed to <a href="/wiki/Vuk_Karad%C5%BEi%C4%87" title="Vuk Karadžić">Vuk Stefanović Karadžić</a>, who updated the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Serbian Cyrillic alphabet">Serbian Cyrillic alphabet</a> by removing certain graphemes no longer represented in the vernacular and introducing graphemes specific to Serbian (i.e. Љ Њ Ђ Ћ Џ Ј), distancing it from Church Slavonic alphabet in use prior to the reform. Today, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Languages_using_Cyrillic" title="Languages using Cyrillic">many languages</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Balkans" title="Languages of the Balkans">Balkans</a>, Eastern Europe, and <a href="/wiki/Eurasiatic_languages" title="Eurasiatic languages">northern Eurasia</a> are written in Cyrillic alphabets.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Letters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cyrillic script spread throughout the East Slavic and some South Slavic territories, being adopted for writing local languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Old_East_Slavic" title="Old East Slavic">Old East Slavic</a>. Its adaptation to local languages produced a number of Cyrillic alphabets, discussed below.
</p>

<p>Capital and lowercase letters were not distinguished in old manuscripts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="349" decoding="async" height="379" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG/220px-Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG/330px-Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meletius_Smotrisky_Cyrillic_Alphabet.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <i>Church Slavonic Grammar</i> of <a href="/wiki/Meletius_Smotrytsky" title="Meletius Smotrytsky">Meletius Smotrytsky</a> (1619)</div></div></div>
<p>Yeri (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ы</span>) was originally a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligature_(typography)" title="Ligature (typography)">ligature</a> of Yer and I (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ъ</span> + <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">І</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ы</span>). <a href="/wiki/Iotation" title="Iotation">Iotation</a> was indicated by ligatures formed with the letter І: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iotated_A_(Cyrillic)" title="Iotated A (Cyrillic)">Ꙗ</a></span> (not an ancestor of modern Ya, Я, which is derived from <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѧ</span>), <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѥ</span>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ю</span> (ligature of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">І</span> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">ОУ</span>), <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѩ</span>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѭ</span>. Sometimes different letters were used interchangeably, for example <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">И</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">І</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ї</span>, as were typographical variants like <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">О</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѻ</span>. There were also commonly used ligatures like <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">ѠТ</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѿ</span>.
</p><p>The letters also had numeric values, based not on Cyrillic alphabetical order, but inherited from the letters' <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals" title="Greek numerals">Greek ancestors</a>.
</p>

<td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ц</span> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1113239643" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-Cyrs" title="Slavonic">Ѧ</span>)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The early Cyrillic alphabet is difficult to represent on computers. Many of the letterforms differed from those of modern Cyrillic, varied a great deal in <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a>, and changed over time. Few fonts include <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a> sufficient to reproduce the alphabet. In accordance with <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> policy, the standard does not include letterform variations or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligature_(typography)" title="Ligature (typography)">ligatures</a> found in manuscript sources unless they can be shown to conform to the Unicode definition of a character.
</p><p>The Unicode 5.1 standard, released on 4 April 2008, greatly improves computer support for the early Cyrillic and the modern <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a> language. In Microsoft Windows, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Segoe_UI" title="Segoe UI">Segoe UI</a> user interface font is notable for having complete support for the archaic Cyrillic letters since Windows 8.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_signs">Currency signs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Currency signs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Currency_sign" title="Currency sign">currency signs</a> have derived from Cyrillic letters:
</p>
<ul><li>The Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Hryvnia_sign" title="Hryvnia sign">hryvnia sign</a> (₴) is from the <a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">cursive</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minuscule" title="Minuscule">minuscule</a> <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_alphabet" title="Ukrainian alphabet">Ukrainian Cyrillic letter</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/He_(Cyrillic)" title="He (Cyrillic)">He</a> (<span style="font-family: times, 'Times New Roman', serif;"><i>г</i></span>).</li>
<li>The Russian <a href="/wiki/Ruble_sign" title="Ruble sign">ruble sign</a> (₽) from the majuscule Р.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstani_som" title="Kyrgyzstani som">Kyrgyzstani som</a> sign (<u>с</u>) from the majuscule С (es)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstani_tenge" title="Kazakhstani tenge">Kazakhstani tenge</a> sign (₸) from Т</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_t%C3%B6gr%C3%B6g" title="Mongolian tögrög">Mongolian tögrög</a> sign (₮) from Т</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Letterforms_and_typography">Letterforms and typography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Letterforms and typography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The development of Cyrillic <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a> passed directly from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> stage to the late <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a>, without a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> phase as in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>. Late Medieval Cyrillic letters (categorized as <a href="/wiki/Vyaz_(Cyrillic_calligraphy)" title="Vyaz (Cyrillic calligraphy)">vyaz'</a> and still found on many <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> inscriptions today) show a marked tendency to be very tall and narrow, with strokes often shared between adjacent letters.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a>, Tsar of Russia, mandated the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_script" title="Civil script">westernized letter forms</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%93%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D1%88%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%84%D1%82" title="ru:Гражданский шрифт">ru</a>) in the early 18th century. Over time, these were largely adopted in the other languages that use the script. Thus, unlike the majority of modern Greek fonts that retained their own set of design principles for lower-case letters (such as the placement of <a href="/wiki/Serif" title="Serif">serifs</a>, the shapes of stroke ends, and stroke-thickness rules, although Greek capital letters do use Latin design principles), modern Cyrillic fonts are much the same as modern Latin fonts of the same font family. The development of some Cyrillic computer typefaces from Latin ones has also contributed to the visual Latinization of Cyrillic type.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lowercase_forms">Lowercase forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lowercase forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_upright-cursive-n.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="86" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Cyrillic_upright-cursive-n.svg/320px-Cyrillic_upright-cursive-n.svg.png" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Letters Ge, De, I, Short I, Em, Te, Tse, Be and Ve in upright (printed) and cursive (handwritten) variants. (Top is set in Georgia font, bottom in Odessa Script.)</div></div></div>
<p>Cyrillic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_letters" title="Capital letters">uppercase</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_case" title="Lower case">lowercase</a> letter forms are not as differentiated as in Latin typography. Upright Cyrillic lowercase letters are essentially <a href="/wiki/Small_caps" title="Small caps">small capitals</a> (with exceptions: Cyrillic ⟨а⟩, ⟨е⟩, ⟨і⟩, ⟨ј⟩, ⟨р⟩, and ⟨у⟩ adopted Western lowercase shapes, lowercase ⟨ф⟩ is typically designed under the influence of Latin ⟨p⟩, lowercase ⟨б⟩, ⟨ђ⟩ and ⟨ћ⟩ are traditional handwritten forms, although a good-quality Cyrillic typeface will still include separate small-caps glyphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cyrillic fonts, as well as Latin ones, have <a href="/wiki/Roman_type" title="Roman type">roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italic_type" title="Italic type">italic</a> types (practically all popular modern fonts include parallel sets of Latin and Cyrillic letters, where many glyphs, uppercase as well as lowercase, are simply shared by both). However, the native font terminology in most Slavic languages (for example, in Russian) does not use the words "roman" and "italic" in this sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Instead, the nomenclature follows German naming patterns:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_alternates.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Cyrillic_alternates.svg/200px-Cyrillic_alternates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Cyrillic_alternates.svg/300px-Cyrillic_alternates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Cyrillic_alternates.svg/400px-Cyrillic_alternates.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_alternates.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alternate variants of lowercase (cursive) Cyrillic letters: Б/б, Д/д, Г/г, И/и, П/п, Т/т, Ш/ш.  <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#9CC2E5; color:black;"> </span> Default Russian (Eastern) forms on the left.</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#F4B083; color:black;"> </span> Alternate Bulgarian (Western) upright forms in the middle.</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD966; color:black;"> </span> Alternate Serbian/Macedonian (Southern) italic forms on the right.</div> <i>See also:</i><br/> <div class="floatleft"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_cursive.svg"><img alt="Cyrillic cursive.svg" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="437" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cyrillic_cursive.svg/75px-Cyrillic_cursive.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cyrillic_cursive.svg/113px-Cyrillic_cursive.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cyrillic_cursive.svg/150px-Cyrillic_cursive.svg.png 2x" width="75"/></a></div> <div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg"><img alt="Special Cyrillics BGDPT.svg" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="301" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg/75px-Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg/113px-Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg/150px-Special_Cyrillics_BGDPT.svg.png 2x" width="75"/></a></div></div></div></div>
<ul><li>Roman type is called <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">pryamoy shrift</i></span></i> ("upright type") – compare with <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Normalschrift</i></span></i> ("regular type") in German</li>
<li>Italic type is called <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">kursiv</i></span></i> ("cursive") or <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">kursivniy shrift</i></span></i> ("cursive type") – from the German word <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Kursive</i></span></i>, meaning italic typefaces and not cursive writing</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">Cursive</a> handwriting is <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">rukopisniy shrift</i></span></i> ("handwritten type") – in German: <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurrentschrift" title="de:Kurrentschrift">Kurrentschrift</a></i></span></i> or <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Laufschrift</i></span></i>, both meaning literally 'running type'</li>
<li>A (mechanically) sloped oblique type of <a href="/wiki/Sans-serif" title="Sans-serif">sans-serif</a> faces is <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">naklonniy shrift</i></span></i> ("sloped" or "slanted type").</li>
<li>A boldfaced type is called <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">poluzhirniy shrift</i></span></i> ("semi-bold type"), because there existed fully boldfaced shapes that have been out of use since the beginning of the 20th century.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italic_and_cursive_forms">Italic and cursive forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Italic and cursive forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Similarly to Latin fonts, italic and cursive types of many Cyrillic letters (typically lowercase; uppercase only for handwritten or stylish types) are very different from their upright roman types. In certain cases, the correspondence between uppercase and lowercase glyphs does not coincide in Latin and Cyrillic fonts: for example, italic Cyrillic <span style="font-family: times, 'Times New Roman', serif; font-size: larger">⟨<i>т</i>⟩</span> is the lowercase counterpart of ⟨<i>Т</i>⟩ not of ⟨<i>М</i>⟩.
</p>
<table border="0" cellpadding="4" cellspacing="1" style="padding:0 .5em .2em; border:1px solid #999; margin:1em 0;">

<p>Note: in some fonts or styles, ⟨<i>д</i>⟩, i.e. the lowercase italic Cyrillic ⟨д⟩, may look like Latin ⟨<i>g</i>⟩, and ⟨<i>т</i>⟩, i.e. lowercase italic Cyrillic ⟨т⟩, may look like small-capital italic ⟨T⟩.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Serbian"></span><span class="anchor" id="Macedonian"></span>
In Standard Serbian, as well as in Macedonian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> some italic and cursive letters are allowed to be different to more closely resemble the handwritten letters. The regular (upright) shapes are generally standardized in <a href="/wiki/Small_caps" title="Small caps">small caps</a> form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>

</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Bulgarian"></span>
In <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_alphabet" title="Bulgarian alphabet">Bulgarian typography</a>, many lowercase letterforms may more closely resemble the cursive forms on the one hand and Latin glyphs on the other hand, e.g. by having an ascender or descender or by using rounded arcs instead of sharp corners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Sometimes, uppercase letters may have a different shape as well, e.g. more triangular, Д and Л, like Greek delta Δ and lambda Λ.
</p>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accessing_variant_forms">Accessing variant forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Accessing variant forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Computer fonts typically default to the Central/Eastern, Russian letterforms, and require the use of <a href="/wiki/OpenType" title="OpenType">OpenType</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_typographic_features" title="List of typographic features">Layout (OTL) features</a> to display the Western, Bulgarian or Southern, Serbian/Macedonian forms. Depending on the choices of the font manufacturer, they may either be automatically activated by the <i>local variant</i> <code>locl</code> feature for text tagged with an appropriate <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-1" title="ISO 639-1">language code</a>, or the author needs to opt-in by activating a <i>stylistic set</i> <code>ss##</code> or <i>character variant</i> <code>cv##</code> feature. These solutions only enjoy partial support and may render with default glyphs in certain software configurations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyrillic_alphabets">Cyrillic alphabets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cyrillic alphabets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic alphabets</a></div>
<p>Among others, Cyrillic is the standard script for writing the following languages:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Slavic languages</b>: <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rusyn_language" title="Rusyn language">Rusyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">Serbo-Croatian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Standard Serbian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Montenegrin_language" title="Montenegrin language">Montenegrin</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a></li>
<li><b>Non-Slavic languages of Russia</b>: <a href="/wiki/Abaza_language" title="Abaza language">Abaza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adyghe_language" title="Adyghe language">Adyghe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bashkir_language" title="Bashkir language">Bashkir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buryat_language" title="Buryat language">Buryat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chechen_language" title="Chechen language">Chechen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuvash_language" title="Chuvash language">Chuvash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzya_language" title="Erzya language">Erzya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ingush_language" title="Ingush language">Ingush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabardian_language" title="Kabardian language">Kabardian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalmyk_Oirat" title="Kalmyk Oirat">Kalmyk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karachay-Balkar_language" title="Karachay-Balkar language">Karachay-Balkar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kildin_Sami_language" title="Kildin Sami language">Kildin Sami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moksha_language" title="Moksha language">Moksha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nogai_language" title="Nogai language">Nogai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ossetian_language" title="Ossetian language">Ossetian</a> (in <a href="/wiki/North_Ossetia%E2%80%93Alania" title="North Ossetia–Alania">North Ossetia–Alania</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romani_orthography#Cyrillic_script" title="Romani orthography">Romani</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakha_language" title="Sakha language">Sakha/Yakut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatar_language" title="Tatar language">Tatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvan_language" title="Tuvan language">Tuvan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">Udmurt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siberian_Yupik_language" title="Siberian Yupik language">Yuit</a> (Yupik)</li>
<li><b>Non-Slavic languages in other countries</b>: <a href="/wiki/Abkhaz_language" title="Abkhaz language">Abkhaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleut_language" title="Aleut language">Aleut</a> (now mostly in church texts), <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a> (to be replaced by Latin script by 2025<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MongolSwitch_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MongolSwitch-40">[40]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_language" title="Kyrgyz language">Kyrgyz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a> (to also be written with traditional <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian script</a> by 2025<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KazSwitch_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-KazSwitch-41">[41]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tlingit_alphabet#Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Tlingit alphabet">Tlingit</a> (now only in church texts), <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">Turkmen</a> (officially replaced by Latin script), <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a> (also officially replaced by Latin script, but still in wide use), <a href="/wiki/Yupik_languages#Writing_systems" title="Yupik languages">Yupik</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<p>The Cyrillic script has also been used for languages of Alaska,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_Europe" title="Slavic Europe">Slavic Europe</a> (except for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Slavs" title="Western Slavs">Western Slavic</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Slavs" title="Southern Slavs">Southern Slavic</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, the languages of <a href="/wiki/Idel-Ural" title="Idel-Ural">Idel-Ural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>.
</p><p>The first alphabet derived from Cyrillic was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abur" title="Abur">Abur</a>, used for the <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi language</a>. Other Cyrillic alphabets include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molodtsov_alphabet" title="Molodtsov alphabet">Molodtsov alphabet</a> for the Komi language and various alphabets for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_languages" title="Caucasian languages">Caucasian languages</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_of_Cyrillic_versus_other_scripts">Usage of Cyrillic versus other scripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Usage of Cyrillic versus other scripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_script">Latin script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Latin script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of languages written in a Cyrillic alphabet have also been written in a <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> (in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Moldova" title="Republic of Moldova">Republic of Moldova</a> until 1989, in the <a href="/wiki/Danubian_Principalities" title="Danubian Principalities">Danubian Principalities</a> throughout the 19th century). After the disintegration of the Soviet Union in 1991, some of the former republics officially shifted from Cyrillic to Latin. The transition is complete in most of Moldova (except the breakaway region of <a href="/wiki/Transnistria" title="Transnistria">Transnistria</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Moldovan_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Moldovan Cyrillic alphabet">Moldovan Cyrillic</a> is official), <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> still uses both systems, and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> has officially begun a transition from Cyrillic to Latin (scheduled to be complete by 2025). The <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> government has mandated that Cyrillic must be used for all public communications in all <a href="/wiki/Federal_subjects_of_Russia" title="Federal subjects of Russia">federal subjects of Russia</a>, to promote closer ties across the federation. This act was controversial for speakers of many Slavic languages; for others, such as <a href="/wiki/Chechen_language" title="Chechen language">Chechen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ingush_language" title="Ingush language">Ingush</a> speakers, the law had political ramifications. For example, the separatist Chechen government mandated a Latin script which is still used by many Chechens.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="1035" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg/440px-Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg/660px-Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg/880px-Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyrillic_alphabet_world_distribution.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with widespread use of the Cyrillic script:<br/> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0b280b; color:white;"> </span> Sole official script</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#44aa00; color:black;"> </span> Co-official with another script (either because the official language is biscriptal, or the state is bilingual)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#55d400; color:black;"> </span> Being replaced with Latin, but is still in official use</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ccffaa; color:black;"> </span> Legacy script for the official language, or large minority use</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#D7D7D7; color:black;"> </span> Cyrillic is not widely used</div></div></div></div>
<p>Standard <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language#Writing_system" title="Serbian language">both the Cyrillic and Latin scripts</a>. Cyrillic is nominally the official script of Serbia's administration according to the Serbian constitution;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> however, the law does not regulate scripts in standard language, or standard language itself by any means. In practice the scripts are equal, with Latin being used more often in a less official capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhuang_alphabet" title="Zhuang alphabet">Zhuang alphabet</a>, used between the 1950s and 1980s in portions of the People's Republic of China, used a mixture of Latin, phonetic, numeral-based, and Cyrillic letters. The non-Latin letters, including Cyrillic, were removed from the alphabet in 1982 and replaced with Latin letters that closely resembled the letters they replaced.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanization">Romanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Romanization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are various systems for <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">Romanization</a> of Cyrillic text, including <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliteration</a> to convey Cyrillic spelling in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> letters, and <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">transcription</a> to convey <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">pronunciation</a>.
</p><p>Standard Cyrillic-to-Latin transliteration systems include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Scientific_transliteration_of_Cyrillic" title="Scientific transliteration of Cyrillic">Scientific transliteration</a>, used in linguistics, is based on the <a href="/wiki/Gaj%27s_Latin_alphabet" title="Gaj's Latin alphabet">Serbo-Croatian Latin alphabet</a>.</li>
<li>The Working Group on Romanization Systems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> recommends different systems for specific languages. These are the most commonly used around the world.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/ISO_9" title="ISO 9">ISO 9</a>:1995, from the International Organization for Standardization.</li>
<li>American Library Association and Library of Congress Romanization tables for Slavic alphabets (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALA-LC_Romanization" title="ALA-LC Romanization">ALA-LC Romanization</a>), used in North American libraries.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BGN/PCGN_Romanization" title="BGN/PCGN Romanization">BGN/PCGN Romanization</a> (1947), United States Board on Geographic Names &amp; Permanent Committee on Geographical Names for British Official Use).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/GOST_16876-71" title="GOST 16876-71">GOST 16876</a>, a now defunct Soviet transliteration standard. Replaced by GOST 7.79, which is <a href="/wiki/ISO_9" title="ISO 9">ISO 9</a> equivalent.</li>
<li>Various <a href="/wiki/Informal_romanizations_of_Cyrillic" title="Informal romanizations of Cyrillic">informal romanizations of Cyrillic</a>, which adapt the Cyrillic script to Latin and sometimes Greek glyphs for compatibility with small character sets.</li></ul>
<p>See also <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Belarusian" title="Romanization of Belarusian">Romanization of Belarusian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Bulgarian" title="Romanization of Bulgarian">Bulgarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Kyrgyz" title="Romanization of Kyrgyz">Kyrgyz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Macedonian" title="Romanization of Macedonian">Macedonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ukrainian" title="Romanization of Ukrainian">Ukrainian</a>.
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyrillization">Cyrillization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cyrillization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Representing other writing systems with Cyrillic letters is called <a href="/wiki/Cyrillization" title="Cyrillization">Cyrillization</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_encoding">Computer encoding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Computer encoding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode">Unicode</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Unicode">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script_in_Unicode" title="Cyrillic script in Unicode">Cyrillic script in Unicode</a></div>
<p>As of Unicode version 15.0, Cyrillic letters, including national and historical alphabets, are encoded across several <a href="/wiki/Unicode_block" title="Unicode block">blocks</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_(Unicode_block)" title="Cyrillic (Unicode block)">Cyrillic</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U0400.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+0400–U+04FF</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_Supplement" title="Cyrillic Supplement">Cyrillic Supplement</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U0500.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+0500–U+052F</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_Extended-A" title="Cyrillic Extended-A">Cyrillic Extended-A</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U2DE0.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+2DE0–U+2DFF</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_Extended-B" title="Cyrillic Extended-B">Cyrillic Extended-B</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/UA640.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+A640–U+A69F</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_Extended-C" title="Cyrillic Extended-C">Cyrillic Extended-C</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U1C80.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+1C80–U+1C8F</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_Extended-D" title="Cyrillic Extended-D">Cyrillic Extended-D</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U1E030.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+1E030–U+1E08F</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phonetic_Extensions" title="Phonetic Extensions">Phonetic Extensions</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/U1D00.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+1D2B, U+1D78</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Combining_Half_Marks" title="Combining Half Marks">Combining Half Marks</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/PDF/UFE20.pdf" rel="nofollow">U+FE2E–U+FE2F</a></li></ul>
<p>The characters in the range U+0400 to U+045F are essentially the characters from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_8859-5" title="ISO 8859-5">ISO 8859-5</a> moved upward by 864 positions. The characters in the range U+0460 to U+0489 are historic letters, not used now. The characters in the range U+048A to U+052F are additional letters for various languages that are written with Cyrillic script.
</p><p>Unicode as a general rule does not include accented Cyrillic letters. A few exceptions include:
</p>
<ul><li>combinations that are considered as separate letters of respective alphabets, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%99" title="Й">Й</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%8E" title="Ў">Ў</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%81" title="Ё">Ё</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%87" title="Ї">Ї</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%83" title="Ѓ">Ѓ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%8C" title="Ќ">Ќ</a> (as well as many letters of non-Slavic alphabets);</li>
<li>two most frequent combinations orthographically required to distinguish <a href="/wiki/Homonym" title="Homonym">homonyms</a> in Bulgarian and Macedonian: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%80" title="Ѐ">Ѐ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%8D" title="Ѝ">Ѝ</a>;</li>
<li>a few Old and New Church Slavonic combinations: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D1%B6" title="Ѷ">Ѷ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D1%BE" title="Ѿ">Ѿ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D1%BC" title="Ѽ">Ѽ</a>.</li></ul>
<p>To indicate stressed or long vowels, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combining_diacritical_mark" title="Combining diacritical mark">combining diacritical marks</a> can be used after the respective letter (for example, <span class="nowrap"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="monospaced">U+0301</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">◌́</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">COMBINING ACUTE ACCENT</span>: е́ у́ э́ etc.).
</p><p>Some languages, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic</a>, are still not fully supported.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Unicode 5.1, released on 4 April 2008, introduces major changes to the Cyrillic blocks. Revisions to the existing Cyrillic blocks, and the addition of Cyrillic Extended A (2DE0 ... 2DFF) and Cyrillic Extended B (A640 ... A69F), significantly improve support for the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">early Cyrillic alphabet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abkhaz_language" title="Abkhaz language">Abkhaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleut_language" title="Aleut language">Aleut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuvash_language" title="Chuvash language">Chuvash</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moksha_language" title="Moksha language">Moksha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Punctuation for Cyrillic text is similar to that used in European Latin-alphabet languages.
</p><p>Other <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encoding</a> systems for Cyrillic:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CP866" title="CP866">CP866</a> – 8-bit Cyrillic character encoding established by <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> also known as GOST-alternative. Cyrillic characters go in their native order, with a "window" for pseudographic characters.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_8859-5" title="ISO/IEC 8859-5">ISO/IEC 8859-5</a> – 8-bit Cyrillic character encoding established by <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KOI8-R" title="KOI8-R">KOI8-R</a> – 8-bit native Russian character encoding. Invented in the USSR for use on Soviet clones of American IBM and DEC computers. The Cyrillic characters go in the order of their Latin counterparts, which allowed the text to remain readable after transmission via a 7-bit line that removed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Most_significant_bit" title="Most significant bit">most significant bit</a> from each byte – the result became a very rough, but readable, Latin transliteration of Cyrillic. Standard encoding of early 1990s for <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> systems and the first Russian Internet encoding.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KOI8-U" title="KOI8-U">KOI8-U</a> – KOI8-R with addition of Ukrainian letters.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIK_Code_page" title="MIK Code page">MIK</a> – 8-bit native Bulgarian character encoding for use in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> <a href="/wiki/DOS" title="DOS">DOS</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Windows-1251" title="Windows-1251">Windows-1251</a> – 8-bit Cyrillic character encoding established by Microsoft for use in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>. The simplest 8-bit Cyrillic encoding – 32 capital chars in native order at 0xc0–0xdf, 32 usual chars at 0xe0–0xff, with rarely used "YO" characters somewhere else. No pseudographics. Former standard encoding in some <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> distributions for Belarusian and Bulgarian, but currently displaced by <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">UTF-8</a>.</li>
<li>GOST-main.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/GB_2312" title="GB 2312">GB 2312</a> – Principally simplified Chinese encodings, but there are also the basic 33 Russian Cyrillic letters (in upper- and lower-case).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/JIS_encoding" title="JIS encoding">JIS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shift_JIS" title="Shift JIS">Shift JIS</a> – Principally Japanese encodings, but there are also the basic 33 Russian Cyrillic letters (in upper- and lower-case).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Keyboard_layouts">Keyboard layouts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Keyboard layouts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_layout#Keyboard_layouts_for_non-Latin_alphabetic_scripts" title="Keyboard layout">Keyboard layouts for non-Latin alphabetic scripts</a></div>
<p>Each language has its own standard <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_layout" title="Keyboard layout">keyboard layout</a>, adopted from <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriters</a>. With the flexibility of computer input methods, there are also transliterating or phonetic/homophonic keyboard layouts made for typists who are more familiar with other layouts, like the common English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QWERTY_keyboard" title="QWERTY keyboard">QWERTY keyboard</a>. When practical Cyrillic keyboard layouts or fonts are unavailable, computer users sometimes use transliteration or look-alike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volapuk_encoding" title="Volapuk encoding">"volapuk" encoding</a> to type in languages that are normally written with the Cyrillic alphabet.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrillic_script&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Name <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">ital'yanskiy shrift</i></span></i> (Italian font) in Russian refers to a particular font family <a class="external text" href="http://citforum.univ.kiev.ua/open_source/fonts/theory/thumbs/ris320.jpg" rel="nofollow">JPG</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070926182512/http://citforum.univ.kiev.ua/open_source/fonts/theory/thumbs/ris320.jpg" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 26 September 2007 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, whereas <i><span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">rimskiy shrift</i></span></i> (roman font) is just a synonym for Latin font, Latin alphabet.</span>
